Second Objection from Iblis's Disobedience and Its Refutation
Objection from Iblis's disobedience and its refutation using his jinn nature and taghlib.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objection that Iblis's disobedience disproves angelic infallibility is refuted by arguing Iblis was from the jinn, and why the matter remains speculative.

Core Idea
Iblis was a jinn, not an angel, so his disobedience does not affect angelic infallibility.

The second objection against angelic infallibility uses the case of Iblis, who disobeyed Allah and was cursed. The objector claims Iblis was an angel, based on Quranic exceptions. This lesson presents the refutation: Iblis was from the jinn, and the exception is due to taghlib. We will also explore the limits of proof in this debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection that Iblis's disobedience proves angels can sin. Note the Quranic evidence used: Iblis is excepted from angels, so he must be one of them.
Notice the two pieces of evidence: exception in surah al-Hijr and inclusion in the command to angels.
2
Examine the refutation
The answer states that Iblis was from the jinn, citing surah al-Kahf. Understand that the exception and command inclusion are explained by taghlib—giving precedence to the majority.
Taghlib is a linguistic principle: treating a group as if it includes an exceptional member for simplicity.
3
Address the counter-argument
Some claim that 'jinn' is a name for a group of angels. The passage argues against this: the context shows jinn nature explains Iblis's disobedience, which angelic nature would not.
The passage says 'jinn' does not fall under 'angel' and the context of rebellion precludes Iblis being an angel.
4
Consider the proponent's evidence
Verses that seem to prove angelic infallibility are presented, e.g., they do not disobey Allah. But the refutation notes these verses are not general enough to cover all angels at all times.
The argument is that these verses are not decisive (qati') in proving absolute infallibility.
5
Understand the conclusion
The matter is not definitively settled; only speculative arguments (zanni) exist. In such theological matters, certainty is required, and conjecture is insufficient.
This highlights the importance of certainty in foundational beliefs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الوجه الثاني: إبليس عاص بترك السجود حتى صار مطروداً ملعوناً وهو من الملائكة بدليل استثنائه منهم في قوله تعالى: ﴿ فسجد الملائكة كلهم أجمعون إلا إبليس ﴾ [الحجر:٣٠]، وبدليل أن قوله تعالى: ﴿ وإذ قلنا للملائكة اسجدوا ﴾ [البقرة: ٣٥]، قد تناوله وإلا لما استحق الذم ولما قيل له: ﴿ وما منعك أن لا تسجد إذ أمرتك ﴾ [الأعراف: ١٢].
الجواب: عن الوجه الثاني: أن إبليس كان من الجن لقوله تعالى: ﴿ كان من الجن ففسق عن أمر ربه ﴾ [الكهف: ٥٠]، وصح الاستثناء وتناوله الأمر للغلبة أي لتغليب الكثير على القليل في إطلاق الاسم كما عرف في وضعه، وكون طائفة من الملائكة مسمين بالجن على ما قيل فلا يكون حينئذ كونه من الجن منافياً لكونه من الملائكة خلاف الظاهر، لأن لفظ الجن ما لا يدخل تحت الملك، مع أن ذكره أي ذكر كونه من الجن في معرض التعليل لاستكباره وعصيانه كما يتبادر من نظم الآية يأباه أي يأبى كونه من الملائكة لأن طبيعة الملك لا تقتضي المعصية أو يأبى كون الجن اسماً لطائفة من الملائكة، وللمثبت الآية الدالة على عصمتهم نحو قوله تعالى: ﴿ ولا يعصون الله ما أمرهم ويفعلون ما يؤمرون ﴾ [التحريم: ٦] وقوله: ﴿ يسبحون الليل والنهار لا يفترون ﴾ [الأنبياء: ٢٠] إذ يعلم منه أنهم لا يعصون وإلا حصل الفتور في التسبيح، وقوله: ﴿ يخافون ربهم من فوقهم ﴾ [النحل: ٥٠] أي فلا يعصونه ويفعلون ما يؤمرون، والجواب إنما يتم ذلك الاستدلال بتلك الآيات إذا ثبت عمومها أعياناً وأزماناً ومعاصي، حتى يثبت بها أن جميعهم مبرؤون عن جميع المعاصي في جميع الأزمنة، ولا قاطع أي في هذا المبحث لا نفياً ولا إثباتاً بل أدلة طرفية ظنية، وإن الظن لا يغني في مثله من المسائل التي يطلب فيها العلم واليقين عن الحق شيئاً.
The second aspect: Iblis is disobedient by refusing to prostrate until he became expelled and cursed, and he is from the angels by evidence of his being excepted from them in His saying: 'So the angels prostrated, all of them together, except Iblis' [al-Hijr:30], and by evidence that His saying: 'And [mention] when We said to the angels, "Prostrate"' [al-Baqara:35] included him, otherwise he would not deserve blame and it would not be said to him: 'What prevented you from prostrating when I commanded you?' [al-A'raf:12]. The answer concerning the second aspect: That Iblis was from the jinn due to His saying: 'He was from the jinn, so he disobeyed the command of his Lord' [al-Kahf:50]. And the exception is valid and the command included him due to overwhelming majority (taghlib), i.e., giving predominance to the many over the few in the application of the name, as is known in its usage. And that a group of angels are called 'jinn' according to what is said, so then his being from the jinn is not incompatible with his being from the angels, contrary to the apparent meaning, because the word 'jinn' does not fall under 'angel'. Moreover, his mention—i.e., the mention of his being from the jinn—in the context of explaining his arrogance and disobedience, as is understood from the arrangement of the verse, precludes it—i.e., precludes his being from the angels, because the nature of an angel does not necessitate disobedience, or precludes that 'jinn' is a name for a group of angels. For the proponent (of angelic infallibility) there are verses indicating their infallibility, such as His saying: 'They do not disobey Allah in what He commands them and they do what they are commanded' [al-Tahrim:6], and His saying: 'They glorify [Him] night and day, never flagging' [al-Anbiya:20], for it is known from this that they do not disobey, otherwise there would be lethargy in glorification, and His saying: 'They fear their Lord above them' [al-Nahl:50], i.e., so they do not disobey Him and they do what they are commanded. The response: That inference by those verses is only complete if their generality with respect to individuals and times and acts of disobedience is established, so that it is proven by them that all of them are free from all acts of disobedience at all times. And there is no decisive proof in this topic, neither for negation nor affirmation; rather there are only speculative arguments. And indeed conjecture does not avail against the truth in such matters in which knowledge and certainty are required.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection and its refutation. The objection is that Iblis, who is considered an angel, committed disobedience. The answer is that Iblis was from the jinn, not an angel. The author discusses the exception in the verse and explains that it is based on taghlib, the principle of treating a group as having a common property to simplify language. Then he addresses the claim that some angels are called jinn, but this is contrary to the apparent meaning because 'jinn' does not fall under 'angel'. Moreover, the context of the verse indicates that Iblis's nature as a jinn is given as the reason for his disobedience, which would not be the case if he were an angel. Finally, the proponent's evidence from verses about angelic obedience is countered by stating that those verses are not general enough to prove absolute infallibility, and that the matter is not decisively proven; the arguments are only speculative.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe that the exception of Iblis in surah al-Hijr proves he was an angel, and thus angels are not infallible.
Correction: The passage explains that Iblis was from the jinn (18:50), and the exception is due to taghlib, a linguistic principle. Therefore, his disobedience does not affect angelic infallibility.

Checkpoint
What is the main argument used to refute the claim that Iblis's disobedience proves angels are not infallible?
A) Iblis was an angel who repented later
B) Iblis was from the jinn, not an angel
C) The angels' infallibility is limited to major sins
D) Iblis's disobedience was only apparent, not real

Key Takeaway
Iblis's disobedience does not disprove angelic infallibility because he was from the jinn, and arguments for or against infallibility remain speculative, lacking decisive proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you saw that even in a well-known issue like Iblis's disobedience, certainty can be elusive. Are there areas in your own life where you claim certainty about something without solid proof? What might that reveal about your humility before Allah?
?
The passage ends by stating that conjecture does not avail against the truth. When you hold a theological position, do you ever stop to ask whether you truly know it from Revelation or are you just repeating what you've heard? How would you feel if you were spreading an opinion as fact?
?
Consider the opponent's desire to disprove angelic infallibility. Is there an area of your faith where you are tempted to doubt because of a single piece of evidence you haven't fully examined? What would it take for you to submit to Allah's wisdom even when you don't have all the answers?

The Taghlib Interpretation of Iblis's Exception
Author applies taghlib to interpret the exception of Iblis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the principle of taghlib resolves the interpretation of Iblis's exception in the verse.

Core Idea
Taghlib explains why mentioning one group suffices for all.

This gloss addresses a subtlety in the verse about the prostration command. Was the exception of Iblis continuous or discontinuous? The author applies the linguistic principle of taghlib to show that mentioning the angels alone includes the jinn, as they were also commanded, and the pronoun covers both groups.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on taghlib
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the linguistic term 'taghlib' and how it is used to explain the exception.
Taghlib means giving predominance to the greater over the lesser.
2
Understand the principle
The principle of taghlib allows mentioning one group to represent a larger category. Here, the angels are mentioned but the jinn are implicitly included.
This is a common rhetorical device.
3
See how the pronoun works
The pronoun 'they prostrated' refers to both groups as if He said 'those commanded prostrated except Iblis'. This shows the exception includes Iblis from a group that includes jinn.
This supports the interpretation that the exception is continuous.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: (للغلبة) هذا بناء على الظاهر الذي هو كون الاستثناء متصلاً وقد يقال : الجن أيضاً كانوا مأمورين مع الملائكة لكنه استغنى بذكر الملائكة عن ذكرهم فإنه إذا علم أن الأكابر مأمورون بالتذلل لأحد والتوسل به علم أن الأصاغر أيضاً مأمورون به والضمير في فسجدوا راجع إلى القبيلتين وكأنه قال : فسجد المأمورون بالسجود إلا إبليس كذا ذكره القاضي . قوله: (مسمين بالجن) روى ابن عباس رضي الله تعالى عنه أن من الملائكة ضرباً يتوالدون يقال : لهم الجن، ومنهم إبليس وقيل : الجن من التجنن وهو التستر وكان الملائكة من طائفة متسترون أي غائبون عن الرؤية لعلو مرتبتهم ورفعة منزلتهم لا يرونهم الملائكة إلى دونهم منزلة ولذلك كانت مسمين بالجن. قوله: (لا) نفياً ولا إثباتاً وأما قصة هاروت وماروت وهي مشهورة. فجوابها أن السحرة كانوا يتلقون الأمور الغائبة من الشياطين ويلقونها بين الناس وكان ذلك يشبه الوحي فأنزلهما الله تعالى ليعلم السحر وكيفيته للناس ليعلموا الفرق بين المعجزة والسحر ولا ذنب فيه كذا اللباب . قوله : ( وإن الظن لا يغني إلخ) قيل : هذا جار في الأدلة الآتية وستعرف وجه الاكتفاء بالظن.
His saying: (For overwhelming majority) This is based on the apparent meaning, which is that the exception is continuous. It may be said that the jinn were also commanded along with the angels, but He dispensed with mentioning them by mentioning the angels, for if it is known that the great ones are commanded to humble themselves before someone and to seek means through him, it is known that the lesser ones are also commanded to do so. And the pronoun in 'they prostrated' refers to both groups, as if He said: 'Those who were commanded to prostrate prostrated, except Iblis.' This is what al-Qadi mentioned. His saying: (called 'jinn') Ibn Abbas, may Allah be pleased with him, narrated that among the angels there is a type that procreates, called jinn, and among them is Iblis. And it is said: jinn is from tajannun, which is concealment, and the angels were a group that were hidden, i.e., absent from sight due to the highness of their rank and the loftiness of their station, not seen by the angels below them, and therefore they were called jinn. His saying: (No) neither negation nor affirmation. As for the story of Harut and Marut, which is famous, its answer is that the sorcerers used to receive hidden matters from the devils and cast them among the people, and that resembled revelation. So Allah sent them down to teach sorcery and its method to the people so that they would know the difference between miracle and sorcery, and there is no sin in that. Thus in al-Lubab. His saying: (And indeed conjecture does not avail, etc.) It is said: This applies to the following evidences, and you will come to know the reason for being content with conjecture.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage consists of four glosses that provide additional explanations. The first note discusses the taghlib interpretation of the exception of Iblis: the command to prostrate included both angels and jinn, but only angels were explicitly mentioned; the pronoun 'they prostrated' refers to both groups. This was mentioned by al-Qadi. The second note cites Ibn Abbas that a group of angels that procreate are called jinn, and Iblis was among them. An alternative etymology derives 'jinn' from tajannun (concealment), referring to angels hidden from lower angels. The third note addresses the famous story of Harut and Marut: their teaching of sorcery was to help people distinguish miracles from sorcery, and there is no sin involved. The fourth note comments that the statement 'conjecture does not avail' applies to the following evidences, implying that the arguments presented before this point are conclusive, while later arguments may be less definitive.

Key Terms
تغليب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The exception of Iblis means Iblis was not originally commanded to prostrate because he was a jinn, not an angel.
Correction: Through taghlib, the command included jinn, and Iblis was among them, so the exception is continuous.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, what does the pronoun 'they prostrated' refer to?
A) Only the angels
B) Only the jinn
C) Both angels and jinn
D) Only Iblis

Key Takeaway
The taghlib principle shows that the command to prostrate included both angels and jinn, and Iblis's exception is continuous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever assume that certain acts of worship or obedience are only for 'great' people (scholars, elders) and not for you? How does the principle of taghlib challenge that assumption in your own life?
?
The command to humble oneself before Allah is for all, regardless of status. When you pray or seek means to Allah, do you truly feel that this command applies to you personally, or do you sometimes excuse yourself as 'lesser'? What would change if you fully internalized that you are commanded equally?

The Naming of a Group of Angels as Jinn
Two explanations for angels being called jinn.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why some angels are called jinn and the two explanations given for this naming.

Core Idea
Jinn are not always separate from angels; some angels are called jinn due to concealment.

This gloss delves into the name 'jinn' as applied to a subset of angels. Two interpretations are presented: one from Ibn Abbas that some angels procreate and are called jinn, and another etymological explanation linking 'jinn' to concealment, as these angels are hidden from lower angels.

Guided Path
1
Read the two explanations
Read the Arabic passage carefully to identify the two separate explanations for why a group of angels are called jinn.
The first explanation is from Ibn Abbas, the second gives an etymology.
2
Analyze the first explanation
Ibn Abbas narrates that there is a type of angels that procreate; they are called jinn, and Iblis is among them. This implies a subclass of angels.
This understanding affects the nature of Iblis.
3
Analyze the second explanation
The second explanation derives 'jinn' from tajannun (concealment). These angels are hidden due to their high rank, and lower angels cannot see them.
This etymology is different from the common understanding of jinn as created from fire.

The Story of Harut and Marut: Teaching Sorcery
Author defends Harut and Marut by explaining the purpose of teaching sorcery.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the purpose of Harut and Marut teaching sorcery as a means to distinguish miracles from sorcery.

Core Idea
Harut and Marut taught sorcery to protect the integrity of prophetic miracles.

The third gloss addresses a potentially troubling story: two angels, Harut and Marut, teaching sorcery. The author clarifies that this was not sinful; rather, it was a divine measure to help people differentiate between true miracles and sorcery, which the devils had been spreading.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on Harut and Marut
Read the Arabic passage about Harut and Marut and its translation. Note the problem they address: sorcerers receiving hidden matters from devils.
The story is famous in Islamic tradition.
2
Understand the problem
Sorcerers used to receive hidden information from devils and present it as revelation, confusing people between miracles and sorcery.
This undermined the authenticity of prophetic miracles.
3
Understand the divine remedy
Allah sent Harut and Marut to teach the method of sorcery so that people could recognize the difference. There is no sin in teaching for this purpose.
The source al-Lubab is cited.

Understanding 'Conjecture Does Not Avail'
Author clarifies the scope of the statement about conjecture.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the statement 'conjecture does not avail' refers to subsequent arguments, indicating a shift in evidentiary strength.

Core Idea
Not all proofs are equal; some arguments rely on conjecture.

The final gloss comments on a key phrase in the text: 'conjecture does not avail.' The author indicates that this applies to the following evidences, implying that the preceding arguments were certain and that the upcoming ones may be based on conjecture. This clarifies the epistemological nature of the discussion.

Guided Path
1
Read the gloss on conjecture
Read the Arabic sentence and its translation. Note the context: the phrase is linked to 'the following evidences'.
The phrase is from the Quran.
2
Understand the epistemic shift
The author says this statement applies to the upcoming arguments, meaning that what precedes is certain, but what follows may be based on conjecture.
This helps the reader assess the strength of different arguments.
3
Connect to the broader discussion
The gloss suggests that the reader will later understand why conjecture is sufficient in some contexts for establishing legal or theological matters.

The first note discusses the taghlib interpretation of the exception of Iblis: the command to prostrate included both angels and jinn, but only angels were explicitly mentioned; the pronoun 'they prostrated' refers to both groups. This was mentioned by al-Qadi. The second note cites Ibn Abbas that a group of angels that procreate are called jinn, and Iblis was among them. An alternative etymology derives 'jinn' from tajannun (concealment), referring to angels hidden from lower angels. The third note addresses the famous story of Harut and Marut: their teaching of sorcery was to help people distinguish miracles from sorcery, and there is no sin involved. The fourth note comments that the statement 'conjecture does not avail' applies to the following evidences, implying that the arguments presented before this point are conclusive, while later arguments may be less definitive.

Key Terms
ظن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The passage says that conjecture is always useless.
Correction: The statement is limited in scope: it applies to the following evidences, not all reasoning.

Checkpoint
According to the gloss, to what does the statement 'conjecture does not avail' apply?
A) All previous arguments
B) The following evidences
C) The story of Harut and Marut
D) The exception of Iblis

Key Takeaway
The phrase 'conjecture does not avail' is specifically about upcoming arguments, not a universal dismissal of conjecture.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter an absolute statement like 'conjecture does not avail' in religious texts, do you tend to apply it universally or pause to consider its specific context? How can you train yourself to examine the scope of such statements before drawing conclusions?
?
Reflect on a time when you dismissed a valid argument because it relied on probability rather than certainty. What did you lose by that dismissal, and how might this lesson encourage you to be more discerning about different levels of evidence?

Definition of al-Ma'ad and Views on Restoring the Non-Existent
Defines al-Ma'ad and enumerates positions on restoring the non-existent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of al-Ma'ad in Islamic theology and the different views on whether the non-existent can be restored.

Core Idea
Al-Ma'ad is the return after death; restoring the non-existent is possible but debated.

This passage from al-Mawaqif introduces the topic of al-Ma'ad (the return), clarifying its linguistic and theological meaning. It then presents a key theological controversy: whether the non-existent can be restored to existence. The author outlines the positions of the Ash'aris, Mu'tazila, and other groups, highlighting the subtle differences in their understanding of the non-existent.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of al-Ma'ad
Read the opening sentences where al-Ma'ad is defined as a verbal noun meaning the return of a thing to its former state. Note the specific meanings in the context of resurrection.
Al-Ma'ad here is not a place or time; it is the act of returning.
2
Identify the two aspects of return
The passage mentions return to existence after annihilation (for those who hold bodies cease to exist) and reassembly after dispersion (for those who hold annihilation is mere separation). Recognize the story of Abraham as scriptural support.
The story of the birds (Quran 2:260) is commonly cited.
3
Compare the theological positions
Notice that Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila consider restoration of the non-existent permissible (ja'iz). However, they differ on the status of the non-existent: Mu'tazila consider it a thing with fixed essence, while Ash'aris say it is nothing. Also note the groups who deny restoration of the non-existent altogether.
Be cautious not to confuse the denial of bodily resurrection with denial of restoring the non-existent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني في المعاد. وفيه مقاصد. المقصد الأول: في إعادة المعدوم. المعاد هاهنا مصدر لا اسم مكان أو زمان، وحقيقة العود توجه الشيء إلى ما كان عليه. والمراد هاهنا الرجوع إلى الوجود بعد الفناء، أو رجوع أجزاء البدن إلى الاجتماع بعد التفرق، وإلى الحياة بعد الممات، والأرواح إلى الأبدان بعد المفارقة. وأما المعاد الروحاني على ما يراه الفلاسفة فمعناه رجوع الأرواح إلى ما كان عليه من التجرد عن البدن واستعمال الآلات. فإن المعاد الجسماني يتوقف عليها عند من يقول بإعدام الأجسام دون من يقول بأن فناءها عبارة عن تفرق أجزائها واختلاط بعضها ببعض، كما يدل عليه قصة إبراهيم عليه الصلاة والسلام في إحياء الطير. وهي جائزة عندنا وعند مشايخ المعتزلة، لكن عندهم المعدوم شيء فإذا عدم الموجود بقي ذاته المخصوصة فأمكن لذلك أن يعاد، وعندنا ينتفي بالكلية مع إمكان الإعادة. خلافاً للفلاسفة والتناسخية المنكرين للمعاد الجسماني، وبعض الكرامية وأبي الحسين البصري ومحمود الخوارزمي من المعتزلة فإن هؤلاء وإن كانوا مسلمين معترفين بالمعاد الجسماني ينكرون إعادة المعدوم، ويقولون: إعادة الأجسام هي جمع أجزائها المتفرقة كما نبهنا عليه.
The second observatory concerning al-ma'ad (the return). And it contains objectives. The first objective: on restoring the non-existent (iʿādat al-maʿdūm). Al-ma'ad here is an infinitive, not a noun of place or time. The reality of return is the turning of a thing to what it was. What is intended here is the return to existence after annihilation, or the return of the parts of the body to assembly after dispersion, and to life after death, and the souls to the bodies after separation. As for the spiritual return according to what the philosophers hold, its meaning is the return of souls to what they were of disengagement from the body and the use of instruments. For bodily resurrection depends upon it (i.e., restoring the non-existent) according to those who say the bodies are annihilated, unlike those who say that their annihilation is the dispersion of their parts and the mixing of some with others, as indicated by the story of Abraham (peace and blessings upon him) in reviving the birds. And it is permissible (jā'iz) according to us (Ash'aris) and according to the leading Mu'tazila, but according to them the non-existent is a thing, so when the existent is annihilated its specific essence remains, thus it is possible for it to be returned. According to us, it is completely negated along with the possibility of restoration. Contrary to the philosophers and the transmigrationists who deny bodily resurrection, and some of the Karramiyya, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi from the Mu'tazila, for these, though they are Muslims acknowledging bodily resurrection, deny the restoration of the non-existent, and say: the restoration of bodies is the gathering of their dispersed parts, as we have pointed out.
Source pages p. 316

Commentary
This passage begins by defining the term 'al-ma'ad' (the return) as used in this context. It clarifies that it is a verbal noun meaning the return of a thing to its former state. It then specifies that in this discussion, the return is either to existence after annihilation (for those who hold that bodies are annihilated), or the reassembly of the body's parts after dispersion, or the return of life to the body and the soul to the body after separation. It also mentions the spiritual return as understood by philosophers. The passage then states that bodily resurrection depends on the possibility of restoring the non-existent, but only for those who hold that bodies are completely annihilated; for those who hold that annihilation is merely dispersion, restoration is just reassembly. The story of Abraham reviving the birds is cited as a scriptural indication. Next, the author presents the theological positions: the Ash'aris and most Mu'tazila consider the restoration of the non-existent permissible (jā'iz). However, there is a subtle difference: the Mu'tazila consider the non-existent to be a fixed thing (shay'), so when an existent is annihilated, its specific essence remains and can be returned. In contrast, the Ash'aris hold that the non-existent is completely negated, but still restoration is possible. Finally, the passage lists those who deny the restoration of the non-existent: the philosophers and transmigrationists (who deny bodily resurrection altogether), and some Muslims who affirm bodily resurrection but reinterpret it as the gathering of dispersed parts, such as some Karramiyya, Abu al-Husayn al-Basri, and Mahmud al-Khwarizmi from the Mu'tazila.

Key Terms
المعاد (al-maʿād)
إعادة المعدوم (iʿādat al-maʿdūm)
الوجود (al-wujūd)
العدم (al-ʿadam)
الجائز (al-jāʾiz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that 'restoration of the non-existent' implies the non-existent is a real thing with some kind of existence.
Correction: The passage explains that for Ash'aris, the non-existent is completely negated (nothing), yet God is able to restore it to existence. The Mu'tazila, however, consider the non-existent a fixed essence, but the Ash'ari position denies that.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'aris, what is the status of the non-existent after annihilation?
A) It remains as a fixed essence.
B) It is completely negated but restoration is possible.
C) It is impossible to restore.
D) It is reassembled from dispersed parts.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ma'ad refers to the return to existence after death, and while most theologians affirm the possibility of restoring the non-existent, they differ on whether the non-existent retains any essence prior to restoration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider that your body will one day be completely annihilated—reduced to nothing—do you truly believe that God can restore you exactly as you were, or do you secretly doubt because your mind cannot conceive of something coming from nothing? What would it take for you to be certain?
?
The Mu'tazila believed the non-existent retains a fixed essence; the Ash'aris said it is absolutely nothing, yet God can still restore it. Which view makes you more aware of your absolute dependence on God's will and power? Why?
?
If you were to die tonight, would you trust that your identity, your soul, and your body will be perfectly restored by God on the Day of Judgment? Or is there a part of you that fears you might be lost forever? How does this passage challenge or confirm that trust?

The Consensus on Bodily Resurrection
States that all religious communities agree on the permissibility of bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all religious communities agree on the permissibility of bodily resurrection and that this forms the starting point for theological discussion.

Core Idea
All religious communities affirm the permissibility of bodily resurrection by consensus.

This brief but powerful statement introduces the second objective of the observatory: bodily resurrection. Before delving into proofs, the author establishes a foundational consensus: every religious community agrees that resurrection is possible. This sets the stage for rational and scriptural arguments.

Guided Path

Consensus on Resurrection and Philosophers' Denial
al-Iji states the universal consensus on the possibility and occurrence of resurrection and notes the philosophers' denial.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that all religious communities unanimously agree on the possibility and occurrence of bodily resurrection, while philosophers deny both — setting the stage for theological defense.

Core Idea
Religious consensus on resurrection vs. philosophical denial

Al-Iji opens his discussion on the eschatological topic of bodily resurrection by noting a remarkable consensus: every religious tradition affirms both the possibility and the actual occurrence of resurrection. However, the philosophers stand apart in denying both. This brief but powerful statement establishes the polemical context for the arguments to come.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic sentence carefully, then read the literal English translation. Notice the concise style of the matn.
2
Identify the two groups
Find the terms 'أهل الملل والشرائع' (people of religions and laws) and 'الفلاسفة' (philosophers). Understand that these are the two contrasting parties.
The first group is broad; the second is specific.
3
Note the dual denial
The verb 'أنكرهما' uses a dual pronoun referring back to 'جوازه ووقوعه' (its possibility and occurrence). The philosophers denied both, not just one.
The dual form is grammatically significant.
4
Reflect on the significance
This consensus among religious communities is presented as almost axiomatic. The philosophers' denial is the exception that demands a response from theologians.
5
Consider the term 'al-falasifa'
Not all philosophers denied bodily resurrection; here it refers specifically to those who did, such as some Peripatetics who rejected bodily return.
This nuances the target of the argument.

Source Text

Concerning the resurrection of bodies, all the people of religions and laws universally agree on its possibility and its occurrence, and the philosophers denied both.

Commentary
This passage is a short introductory statement that sets the stage. It asserts that all religious communities (ahl al-milal wa al-shara'i') unanimously agree that bodily resurrection is possible and will actually occur. The philosophers (al-falasifa) dissented by denying both the possibility and the occurrence. This establishes the polemical context: the theologians must defend the resurrection against philosophical objections.

The Cannibalism Objection: A Rational Dilemma
Al-Iji presents the first objection: the cannibalism dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational dilemma that cannibalism poses for the doctrine of bodily resurrection as numerically identical bodies.

Core Idea
The cannibalism dilemma shows why identity of matter cannot be guaranteed at resurrection.

This lesson presents the first of two arguments used by deniers of bodily resurrection. The cannibalism scenario creates a logical contradiction if resurrection requires the exact same physical parts. Understanding this objection is essential because it forces theologians to refine their concept of what it means for a body to be 'the same.'

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the use of parentheses for explanatory insertions.
2
Identify the two premises of the dilemma
The argument sets up a scenario and then presents two possibilities. Find and understand each premise.
3
Understand the impossibility of a part being in two bodies simultaneously
The dilemma hinges on the impossibility of one part existing in two distinct persons at the same time. Reflect on why this is impossible.
This is a core logical principle.
4
Grasp the conclusion that identical bodily resurrection is impossible
The conclusion is that not all bodies can be returned as their very selves. Consider the implications for the doctrine of resurrection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المنكر بوجهين: الأول : لو أكل إنسان إنساناً بحيث صار المأكول ( أي بعضه ) جزءاً منه ( أي من الآكل فلو أعاد الله ذينك الإنسانين بعينهما ( فتلك الأجزاء ) التي كانت للمأكول ثم صارت للآكل (إما أن تعاد فيهما ) أي في كل واحد منهما ( وهو محال ( لاستحالة أن يكون جزء واحد بعينه في آن واحد في شخصين متباينين ( أو ) يعاد ( في أحدهما وحده فلا يكون الآخر معادا بعينه ( والمقدر خلافه فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها قوله : ( فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها كما زعمتم قيل : هذا إنما يتم لو كان المعاد هو المبتدأ بعينه ونحن لا نقطع بذلك، ولا برهان عليه قطعياً كما سيصرح به بل يجوز أن يكون الإعادة بالمثل بحيث لا يمتاز عن الأول عند الحس ويقال هو هو، وعلى هذا لا يتم الدليل، فإن قلت: فحينئذ لا يكون المثاب والمعاقب هو المطيع والعاصي بل شخصين آخرين، وهذا باطل عقلاً قلنا : المطيع والعاصي والمثاب والمعاقب هي النفس لا غير والبدن مجرد آلة في ذلك وتغاير الآلتين لا يوجب تغاير ذي الآلة. وقد يقال : يلزم من هذا أن يكون المثاب والمعاقب بالذات والآلام الجسمانيتين غير من أطاع وعصى لأن الروح المجرد لا يجازي بها.
The denier argued with two points: The first: If a human eats a human so that the eaten (i.e., part of him) becomes a part of him (i.e., of the eater), then if God were to return those two humans as their very selves, those parts that belonged to the eaten and then became of the eater: either they are returned in both (i.e., in each one of them) which is absurd because it is impossible for one identical part to be at one moment in two distinct persons, or they are returned in one of them alone, and then the other is not returned as his very self, and the assumption is contrary. So it is established that it is not possible to return all bodies as their very selves. His statement: 'So it is established that it is not possible to return all bodies as their very selves as you claim.' It is said: This is only valid if the recreated is the very same as the initial, but we do not definitively hold that, nor is there a conclusive proof for it as he will state. Rather, it is permissible that the recreation be by a similar (body) so that it is not distinguished from the first by sense, and it is said 'it is it.' On this, the argument does not hold. If you say: Then the rewarder and punished one would not be the obedient and disobedient but two other persons, and this is rationally invalid. We say: The obedient and disobedient, and the rewarder and punished, are the soul alone, and the body is merely an instrument in that. The difference of the two instruments does not necessitate the difference of the possessor of the instrument. And it may be said: This entails that the one rewarded and punished with physical pleasures and pains is other than the one who obeyed and disobeyed, because the immaterial soul is not requited with them.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the famous cannibalism objection and the response. The objector argues: if one human eats another, the consumed parts become part of the eater. When both are resurrected as identical to their original selves, those parts cannot be simultaneously present in two bodies. They must be either in both (impossible) or in only one, leaving the other body non-identical. Thus universal bodily resurrection of identical bodies is impossible. The response (from the commentator) denies the premise that resurrection must be with identical bodies. Instead, it is permissible that the recreated body be similar (mithl) to the original, indistinguishable by the senses. Thus the dilemma does not apply. The further objection that then the same person is not rewarded is answered by saying that the soul is the true self, and the body is merely an instrument. The change of instrument does not change the person. However, a contrary opinion is mentioned that physical rewards require the same body. This shows the complexity of the issue.

Key Terms
أكل إنسان
المأكول
الآكل
إعادة بأعيانها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the dilemma presented in the cannibalism objection?
A) Parts cannot be in two bodies at once.
B) Bodies cannot be resurrected.
C) God cannot recreate bodies.
D) The eaten person loses identity.

Key Takeaway
The cannibalism objection challenges the possibility of resurrecting numerically identical bodies when material parts have been shared.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God resurrects you with a body that is not made of the exact atoms you had in this life, would you still be accountable for your deeds? Why does your answer matter for your daily choices?
?
The cannibalism objection suggests that your identity cannot be tied to your physical parts. What does that mean for how you treat your body today? Does your body define you, or is there something more?
?
If resurrection is possible even when material parts are lost or shared, how does that strengthen your trust in God's justice? How should that trust change the way you live right now?

The Response: Resurrection by Similarity and the Soul as the True Self
The commentator responds to the cannibalism objection with the concepts of resurrection by similarity and the soul as the true self.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the commentator's response to the cannibalism objection: resurrection by similarity and the soul as the true self.

Core Idea
Resurrection need not be with identical bodies; a similar body suffices, and the soul is the person.

After presenting the objection, the commentator offers a multi-layered response. First, he denies the premise that resurrection must be with numerically identical bodies. Instead, it is permissible that the recreated body be similar (mithl) to the original, indistinguishable by the senses. He then addresses the objection that this would change the identity of the person by arguing that the true self is the soul, not the body. This lesson unpacks these responses.

Guided Path
1
Read the response passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the different voices: the objector's last attempt and the commentator's reply.
The passage contains a back-and-forth argument.
2
Understand the denial of the need for identical bodies
The commentator says there is no definitive proof that resurrection must be with exactly the same body. This is a key turning point.
3
Grasp the concept of resurrection by similarity (mithl)
Resurrection by a similar body that is indistinguishable by sense is presented as permissible. Consider how this diffuses the dilemma.
This avoids the problem of shared parts.
4
Note the objection about identity and the reply that the soul is the person
If the body is different, the objector argues, the rewarder/punished is a different person. The reply: the soul is the true self; the body is just an instrument.
This is a soul-body dualism argument.
5
Consider the alternative opinion about physical rewards
The passage ends with a note that some say physical rewards require the same body. Reflect on why this complicates the response.
This shows the issue is not fully resolved.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المنكر بوجهين: الأول : لو أكل إنسان إنساناً بحيث صار المأكول ( أي بعضه ) جزءاً منه ( أي من الآكل فلو أعاد الله ذينك الإنسانين بعينهما ( فتلك الأجزاء ) التي كانت للمأكول ثم صارت للآكل (إما أن تعاد فيهما ) أي في كل واحد منهما ( وهو محال ( لاستحالة أن يكون جزء واحد بعينه في آن واحد في شخصين متباينين ( أو ) يعاد ( في أحدهما وحده فلا يكون الآخر معادا بعينه ( والمقدر خلافه فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها قوله : ( فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها كما زعمتم قيل : هذا إنما يتم لو كان المعاد هو المبتدأ بعينه ونحن لا نقطع بذلك، ولا برهان عليه قطعياً كما سيصرح به بل يجوز أن يكون الإعادة بالمثل بحيث لا يمتاز عن الأول عند الحس ويقال هو هو، وعلى هذا لا يتم الدليل، فإن قلت: فحينئذ لا يكون المثاب والمعاقب هو المطيع والعاصي بل شخصين آخرين، وهذا باطل عقلاً قلنا : المطيع والعاصي والمثاب والمعاقب هي النفس لا غير والبدن مجرد آلة في ذلك وتغاير الآلتين لا يوجب تغاير ذي الآلة. وقد يقال : يلزم من هذا أن يكون المثاب والمعاقب بالذات والآلام الجسمانيتين غير من أطاع وعصى لأن الروح المجرد لا يجازي بها.
The denier argued with two points: The first: If a human eats a human so that the eaten (i.e., part of him) becomes a part of him (i.e., of the eater), then if God were to return those two humans as their very selves, those parts that belonged to the eaten and then became of the eater: either they are returned in both (i.e., in each one of them) which is absurd because it is impossible for one identical part to be at one moment in two distinct persons, or they are returned in one of them alone, and then the other is not returned as his very self, and the assumption is contrary. So it is established that it is not possible to return all bodies as their very selves. His statement: 'So it is established that it is not possible to return all bodies as their very selves as you claim.' It is said: This is only valid if the recreated is the very same as the initial, but we do not definitively hold that, nor is there a conclusive proof for it as he will state. Rather, it is permissible that the recreation be by a similar (body) so that it is not distinguished from the first by sense, and it is said 'it is it.' On this, the argument does not hold. If you say: Then the rewarder and punished one would not be the obedient and disobedient but two other persons, and this is rationally invalid. We say: The obedient and disobedient, and the rewarder and punished, are the soul alone, and the body is merely an instrument in that. The difference of the two instruments does not necessitate the difference of the possessor of the instrument. And it may be said: This entails that the one rewarded and punished with physical pleasures and pains is other than the one who obeyed and disobeyed, because the immaterial soul is not requited with them.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents the famous cannibalism objection and the response. The objector argues: if one human eats another, the consumed parts become part of the eater. When both are resurrected as identical to their original selves, those parts cannot be simultaneously present in two bodies. They must be either in both (impossible) or in only one, leaving the other body non-identical. Thus universal bodily resurrection of identical bodies is impossible. The response (from the commentator) denies the premise that resurrection must be with identical bodies. Instead, it is permissible that the recreated body be similar (mithl) to the original, indistinguishable by the senses. Thus the dilemma does not apply. The further objection that then the same person is not rewarded is answered by saying that the soul is the true self, and the body is merely an instrument. The change of instrument does not change the person. However, a contrary opinion is mentioned that physical rewards require the same body. This shows the complexity of the issue.

المعاقب

Key Takeaway
The commentator preserves bodily resurrection by allowing for similarity and identifying the soul as the enduring self.

?
Your true self is your soul, and your body is an instrument. Are you treating your body as a mere tool for obedience to Allah, or do you live as if your body is your ultimate identity? What one change can you make today to align your bodily habits with this truth?
?
The commentator allows for a similar body in resurrection because exact identity is not proven. Does this give you peace about the Day of Judgment, or does it unsettle your certainty? How does your trust in Allah’s wisdom shape your acceptance of mysteries like this?

Proof of the Possibility of Bodily Resurrection
Author proves possibility of bodily resurrection using God's knowledge and power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof for the possibility of bodily resurrection, based on the intrinsic possibility of reassembling parts and God's perfect knowledge and power.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is possible because God knows every part and can restore them.

This lesson presents the core argument for the possibility of bodily resurrection. The author, a Sunni theologian, demonstrates that reassembling scattered body parts is not impossible in itself, and God’s attributes of knowledge and power guarantee its actualization. The passage also clarifies the position of philosophers who deny physical resurrection, affirming instead a spiritual one in a world of similitudes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim and opponents
Read the opening lines that state the topic and note that the philosophers denied both the resurrection and its occurrence.
The philosophers are the main opponents here.
2
Follow the argument for intrinsic possibility
Consider the reasoning: gathering scattered parts and restoring their specific composition is intrinsically possible, even if the parts have been annihilated, because restoring the non-existent is permissible.
This is based on the principle of 'i'adat al-ma'dum' (restoration of the non-existent).
3
See how God's attributes guarantee actualization
Note that God's knowledge encompasses all parts and His power extends to all possible things. Together with the receiver's capability, this makes resurrection definitely possible.
4
Understand the philosophers' alternative view
Read the marginal note to see how philosophers reinterpret resurrection as spiritual, not physical, in a world of similitudes.
This clarifies the nature of the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثاني : في حشر الأجساد ووقوعه وأنكرهما ،الفلاسفة، أما الجواز فلان جمع الأجزاء على ما كانت عليه وإعادة التأليف المخصوص فيها أمر (ممكن لذاته ( كما مر ) وذلك أن الأجزاء المتفرقة المختلطة بغيرها قابلة للجمع بلا ريبة وإن فرض أنها عدمت جاز إعادتها ثم جمعها وإعادة ذلك التأليف فيها لما عرفت من جواز إعادة المعدوم ) والله ) سبحانه وتعالى عالم بتلك الأجزاء ) وأنها لأي بدن من الأبدان ) قادر على جمعها وتأليفها لما بينا من عموم علمه تعالى لجميع المعلومات ( وقدرته ) على جميع الممكنات ( وصحة القبول ) من القابل ( والفعل ) من الفاعل ) توجب الصحة)، أي صحة الوقوع وجوازه (قطعاً) وذلك هو المطلوب . قوله : ( وأنكرهما الفلاسفة الحكماء القائلون بعالم المثل يقولون بالجنة والنار وسائر ما ورد به الشرع لكن قالوا بها في عالم المثل ولم يقولوا بأنها من المحسوسات كما قال به الإسلاميون والأكثرون من الفلاسفة يجعلونها من قبيل اللذات والآلام العقلية .
Second Observatory - Second Objective: On the Resurrection of Bodies and Its Occurrence. The philosophers denied it. As for the possibility, it is because gathering the parts as they were and restoring the specific composition in them is an (intrinsically possible matter (as has passed). And that is because the scattered parts mixed with others are capable of being gathered without doubt, and if it is assumed that they have been annihilated, it is permissible to restore them, then gather them and restore that composition in them, because you have known the permissibility of restoring the non-existent. And God, exalted and high, is knowing of those parts and that they belong to any body of the bodies, and is powerful over gathering them and composing them, because we have shown the generality of His knowledge of all known things and His power over all possible things. And the soundness of acceptance from the receiver and action from the agent necessitates soundness, i.e., the soundness of occurrence and its possibility, definitely. And that is what is sought. Statement: 'And the philosophers denied them'—the wise ones who affirm the world of similitudes. They affirm Paradise and Hell and everything revealed by the Law, but they affirm them in the world of similitudes and do not say they are sensory, as the Muslims say. Most philosophers consider them as intellectual pleasures and pains.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the possibility of bodily resurrection. The author first states the topic: the resurrection of bodies and its occurrence, and notes that the philosophers denied both. Then he argues for possibility: reassembling the body's parts and restoring their specific composition is intrinsically possible. Even if the parts have been annihilated, they can be restored because restoring the non-existent is permissible. God is all-knowing and all-powerful, so He knows which parts belong to which body and can gather them. The necessary conditions for any action—the receiver's capability and the agent's power—are present, so the resurrection is definitely possible. The marginal note explains the philosophers' denial: they affirm a spiritual resurrection in a world of similitudes, not a physical one. This clarifies the opposition the author is addressing.

Proof of Bodily Resurrection from Revelation
Sanusi proves bodily resurrection from the Prophet's clear reports.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that bodily resurrection is proven from the clear, numerous reports of the truthful Prophet, making it a necessary doctrine of Islam.

Core Idea
Bodily resurrection is a necessary part of Islam because the truthful Prophet reported it in texts that cannot be allegorized.

The author now turns to the actual occurrence of bodily resurrection. While its possibility was established rationally, its actuality is known from revelation. The Prophet Muhammad, whose truthfulness is proven by decisive proofs, reported the resurrection in countless verses and hadiths so explicit that they admit no allegorical interpretation. As a result, belief in bodily resurrection is a necessary part of the upright religion, and those who allegorize these texts to spiritual resurrection alone are denying what is essential to Islam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Notice the flow from the proof of the Prophet's truthfulness to the conclusion.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا تقبل التأويل' – this is key.
2
Identify the two-part argument
The passage makes two moves: (1) The truthful Prophet informed about bodily resurrection in clear terms, and (2) Therefore it is a necessary part of religion. Find these in the text.
3
Reflect on the refutation
The author rejects those who interpret these texts as referring only to spiritual matters. Why is this rejection so strong? Because it denies a 'necessity of the religion.'
Consider how this connects to the principle that everything the truthful one says is true.

وأما الوقوع فلان الصادق) الذي علم صدقه بأدلة قاطعة ( أخبر عنه في مواضع لا تحصى بعبارات لا تقبل التأويل حتى صار معلوماً بالضرورة كونه من (الدين) القويم والسراط المستقيم فمن أراد تأويلها بالأمور الراجعة إلى النفوس الناطقة فقط فقد كابر بإنكار ما هو من ضرورات ذلك الدين ( وكل ما أخبر به الصادق فهو حق

And everything that the truthful one informed about is true.

Commentary
This passage is a proof of the actual occurrence of bodily resurrection from revelation. The author argues that the Prophet Muhammad (the truthful one) has reported the resurrection in numerous verses and hadiths that are so clear they cannot be interpreted allegorically. As a result, belief in bodily resurrection is a necessary part of Islam. Anyone who allegorizes these texts to refer only to spiritual resurrection (as some philosophers do) is rejecting a clear doctrine of the religion. The underlying principle is that everything the truthful Prophet reports must be true, so the resurrection must occur.

The Cannibalism Objection
Objection and two preliminary responses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the cannibalism objection to bodily resurrection and two preliminary counterarguments based on resurrection by likeness and the role of the soul.

Core Idea
The cannibalism scenario challenges bodily resurrection but can be answered without resorting to material identity.

This lesson presents the first major objection against bodily resurrection: the cannibalism scenario. The objector argues that if one person eats another, simultaneous restoration of both with the same parts is impossible. We will explore the objection and two preliminary responses: that the scenario is unnecessary given natural processes, and that resurrection may be by likeness rather than exact identity.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the Arabic passage and translation carefully. Notice how the objector sets up the dilemma: the eaten person's parts become the eater's, so God cannot restore both individuals with the same parts.
The objector assumes that resurrection requires the very same material parts.
2
Examine the first counterargument
The note points out that the cannibalism scenario is unnecessary because natural processes already mix bodies; thus the objection is not special.
This counters the premise that cannibalism creates a unique problem.
3
Examine the second counterargument
The second note argues that resurrection could be by likeness, not exact material identity. It also asserts that the soul is the locus of reward and punishment, not the body.
This shifts the focus from physical parts to the enduring soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
احتج المنكر بوجهين: الأول : لو أكل إنسان إنساناً بحيث صار المأكول ( أي بعضه ) جزءاً منه ( أي من الآكل فلو أعاد الله ذينك الإنسانين بعينهما ( فتلك الأجزاء ) التي كانت للمأكول ثم صارت للآكل (إما أن تعاد فيهما ) أي في كل واحد منهما ( وهو محال ( لاستحالة أن يكون جزء واحد بعينه في آن واحد في شخصين متباينين ( أو ) يعاد ( في أحدهما وحده فلا يكون الآخر معادا بعينه ( والمقدر خلافه فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها
6
قوله : ( أو أكل إنسان إنساناً إلخ قيل: لا حاجة إلى هذا الفرض فإنك إذا تأملت ظاهر التربة المعمورة علمت أن ترابها جثث الموتى قد حصل منها النبات وأكلها الدواب وأكلناها وأيضاً قد زرع فيها وغرس ثم حصلت منها الفواكه .
*
قوله : ( فثبت أنه لا يمكن إعادة جميع الأبدان بأعيانها كما زعمتم قيل : هذا إنما يتم لو كان المعاد هو المبتدأ بعينه ونحن لا نقطع بذلك، ولا برهان عليه قطعياً كما سيصرح به بل يجوز أن يكون الإعادة بالمثل بحيث لا يمتاز عن الأول عند الحس ويقال هو هو، وعلى هذا لا يتم الدليل، فإن قلت: فحينئذ لا يكون المثاب والمعاقب هو المطيع والعاصي بل شخصين آخرين، وهذا باطل عقلاً قلنا : المطيع والعاصي والمثاب والمعاقب هي النفس لا غير والبدن مجرد آلة في ذلك وتغاير الآلتين لا يوجب تغاير ذي الآلة. وقد يقال : يلزم من هذا أن يكون المثاب والمعاقب بالذات والآلام الجسمانيتين غير من أطاع وعصى لأن الروح المجرد لا يجازي بها.

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثاني : في حشر الأجساد
من
٣٢٣
كما زعمتم . ( الجواب : إن المعاد إنما هو الأجزاء الأصلية وهي الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره لا جميع الأجزاء ) على الإطلاق ) وهذه ) أي الأجزاء الأصلية التي كانت للإنسان المأكول ( في الأكل فضل فإنا نعلم أن الإنسان باق مدة عمره وأجزاء الغذاء تتوارد عليه وتزول عنه)، وإذا كانت فضلا فيه لم يجب إعادتها في الآكل بل في المأكول .
قوله : (وهي الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخر قيل : الأظهر أن يقال : وهي الأجزاء الحاصلة في أول الفطرة أي أول تعلق الروح بالبدن مما يتعلق به بدونه عادة لأن وجود أجزاء في البدن باقية من أول العمر إلى آخره في حيز المنع ، نعم يعلم كل أحد ببداهته أن ذاته من أول عمره إلى آخره باق بعينه ولا يلزم من ذلك أن ذلك الباقي أجزاءه من بدنه لجواز أن يكون خارجاً عنه . قوله : (لم يجب إعادتها إلى الأكل بل في المأكول فإن قيل : يجوز أن تصير تلك الأجزاء منياً في الأكل ويحصل منه مولود فتكون الأجزاء الأصلية من المأكول أجزاء أصلية لذلك المولود، فيعود المحذور، قلنا: لافساد في الجواز بل في الوقوع فلعل الله تعالى يحفظ الأجزاء الأصلية لشخص من أن يصير أجزاء أصلية لشخص آخر على أنه يجوز أن تكون الأجزاء الأصلية التي هي الإنسان في الحقيقة تقبضها الملائكة بأمر الله تعالى عند حضور الموت، فلا يتعلق بها الأكل ولا يختلط بالتراب ولا يحصل منها النبات والثمار والحبوب وقد يقال : لو سلم تولد المولود من الأجزاء الأصلية للأكول فلا دليل قطعياً على كونها أجزاء أصلية للمولود محشورة معه لجواز أن تكون أجزاؤه الأصلية الأجزاء الهبائية التي ينشرها الملك على الجزء المنوي كما ورد في الخبر الصحيح وأيضاً اللازم جزء لروح مع جزء من البدن أي جزء كان .
The denier objected with two points. The first: If one person eats another such that the eaten (part of him) becomes a part of the eater, then if God were to restore those two human beings as they were, those parts that belonged to the eaten and then became the eater's would either be restored in both—which is impossible because one and the same part cannot be at one instant in two distinct persons—or be restored in one of them alone, and then the other would not be restored as it was, contrary to the assumption. Thus it is established that it is impossible to restore all bodies as they were.
Statement: 'Or one person eating another etc.' It is said: There is no need for this hypothetical scenario, for if you consider the appearance of the inhabited earth, you will know that its soil consists of the corpses of the dead, from which plants grow, and animals eat them, and we eat them, and also seeds are planted and fruits are produced.
Statement: 'Thus it is established that it is impossible to restore all bodies as they were, as you claim.' It is said: This argument would be valid only if the resurrected body is the very same as the original, and we do not assert that decisively, nor is there a decisive proof for it, as will be clarified. Rather, it is possible that the resurrection is by way of a likeness such that it is indistinguishable from the first by the senses and is said to be it. On this basis, the argument does not hold. If you say: Then the rewarded and punished would not be the obedient and disobedient but other persons, and this is rationally invalid. We say: The obedient and disobedient and the rewarded and punished are the soul alone, and the body is merely an instrument in that, and the difference of the two instruments does not necessitate the difference of the possessor of the instrument. And it may be said: This would entail that the one rewarded and punished with bodily pleasures and pains is other than the one who obeyed and disobeyed, because the pure soul cannot be recompensed with them.
[Page 323] As you claim. (The answer: The restored is only the original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, not all parts absolutely. And these (i.e., the original parts that belonged to the eaten person) are surplus in the eater, for we know that a human remains throughout his life while the parts of food come to him and leave him. When they are surplus in him, they must not be restored in the eater but in the eaten.
Statement: 'They are the parts that remain from the beginning of life to the end.' It is said: The more apparent view is that they are the parts that arise at the first creation, i.e., the first attachment of the soul to the body, which the soul normally depends on, because the existence of parts in the body that remain from the beginning of life to the end is a matter of denial. Rather, everyone instinctively knows that his self from the beginning of his life to the end remains the very same, but it does not follow that the remaining thing is the parts of his body, because it could be external to it.
Statement: 'They must not be restored in the eater but in the eaten.' If it is said: It is possible that those parts become semen in the eater and a child is born from him, so that the original parts of the eaten become original parts of that child, and the problem returns. We say: There is no problem with possibility but with actual occurrence. Perhaps God preserves the original parts of a person from becoming original parts of another person. Moreover, it is possible that the original parts that are truly the human are seized by the angels by God's command at the time of death, so they are not affected by eating, nor mixed with soil, nor do plants and fruits come from them. And it may be said: Even if we grant that the child is generated from the original parts of the eaten, there is no decisive proof that they are original parts of the child to be resurrected with him, for it is possible that his original parts are the dust-like parts that the angel spreads over the seminal part, as is reported in authentic hadith. Also, the necessary thing is a part for the soul with a part of the body, any part.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first objection against bodily resurrection. The objector argues using a cannibalism scenario: if one person eats another, the eaten person's parts become part of the eater, so God cannot restore both persons with the same parts. The author's answer is multi-layered. First, the marginal note points out that the scenario is unnecessary because in nature, corpses become soil and plants, etc. Second, the note suggests that resurrection may be by likeness, not exact identity, so the objection fails. Third, the main answer states that only the original parts (present from birth) are restored; later acquired parts are not essential. The commentary further discusses what 'original parts' means: they may be the parts present at the first creation, or the self that remains identical throughout life. Even if a child is born from those parts, God can prevent them from becoming original parts of the child, or the true original parts are taken by angels at death. The objection is adequately refuted.

إعادة المعدوم
المثاب والمعاقب
النفس

Misconception: Bodily resurrection requires the exact same material parts as in life.
Correction: The passage argues that resurrection could be by likeness, and that the soul is the true subject of reward and punishment, making exact material identity unnecessary.

?

The Answer of Original Parts
Main answer and subsequent clarifications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the response that only the original parts of the body are restored, and how this resolves the cannibalism objection.

Core Idea
Only the original parts, not all parts, are restored, so the eaten person's original parts are preserved for him.

This lesson presents the main answer to the cannibalism objection: the resurrection concerns only the original parts that remain from the beginning of life. These parts are not affected by digestion and remain in the eater as surplus. We will also explore further nuances, including the possibility of angels preserving these parts and the role of the soul.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer
Read the passage where the author states that only the original parts are restored. Notice the distinction between original and acquired parts.
The eaten person's original parts are 'surplus' in the eater and will be returned to him.
2
Understand what 'original parts' means
The commentary clarifies that original parts may be those present at the beginning of life or the parts that the soul depends on. It emphasizes that the self remains identical throughout life.
This avoids the requirement that all material parts remain unchanged.
3
Consider further objections
The passage addresses the possibility that original parts could become semen and form a new person. The response is that God can preserve them, or they may be taken by angels at death.
The argument is that actual occurrence is what matters, not mere possibility.

The denier objected with two points. The first: If one person eats another such that the eaten (part of him) becomes a part of the eater, then if God were to restore those two human beings as they were, those parts that belonged to the eaten and then became the eater's would either be restored in both—which is impossible because one and the same part cannot be at one instant in two distinct persons—or be restored in one of them alone, and then the other would not be restored as it was, contrary to the assumption. Thus it is established that it is impossible to restore all bodies as they were.
Statement: 'Or one person eating another etc.' It is said: There is no need for this hypothetical scenario, for if you consider the appearance of the inhabited earth, you will know that its soil consists of the corpses of the dead, from which plants grow, and animals eat them, and we eat them, and also seeds are planted and fruits are produced.
Statement: 'Thus it is established that it is impossible to restore all bodies as they were, as you claim.' It is said: This argument would be valid only if the resurrected body is the very same as the original, and we do not assert that decisively, nor is there a decisive proof for it, as will be clarified. Rather, it is possible that the resurrection is by way of a likeness such that it is indistinguishable from the first by the senses and is said to be it. On this basis, the argument does not hold. If you say: Then the rewarded and punished would not be the obedient and disobedient but other persons, and this is rationally invalid. We say: The obedient and disobedient and the rewarded and punished are the soul alone, and the body is merely an instrument in that, and the difference of the two instruments does not necessitate the difference of the possessor of the instrument. And it may be said: This would entail that the one rewarded and punished with bodily pleasures and pains is other than the one who obeyed and disobeyed, because the pure soul cannot be recompensed with them.
[Page 323] As you claim. (The answer: The restored is only the original parts that remain from the beginning of life to its end, not all parts absolutely. And these (i.e., the original parts that belonged to the eaten person) are surplus in the eater, for we know that a human remains throughout his life while the parts of food come to him and leave him. When they are surplus in him, they must not be restored in the eater but in the eaten.
Statement: 'They are the parts that remain from the beginning of life to the end.' It is said: The more apparent view is that they are the parts that arise at the first creation, i.e., the first attachment of the soul to the body, which the soul normally depends on, because the existence of parts in the body that remain from the beginning of life to the end is a matter of denial. Rather, everyone instinctively knows that his self from the beginning of his life to the end remains the very same, but it does not follow that the remaining thing is the parts of his body, because it could be external to it.
Statement: 'They must not be restored in the eater but in the eaten.' If it is said: It is possible that those parts become semen in the eater and a child is born from him, so that the original parts of the eaten become original parts of that child, and the problem returns. We say: There is no problem with possibility but with actual occurrence. Perhaps God preserves the original parts of a person from becoming original parts of another person. Moreover, it is possible that the original parts that are truly the human are seized by the angels by God's command at the time of death, so they are not affected by eating, nor mixed with soil, nor do plants and fruits come from them. And it may be said: Even if we grant that the child is generated from the original parts of the eaten, there is no decisive proof that they are original parts of the child to be resurrected with him, for it is possible that his original parts are the dust-like parts that the angel spreads over the seminal part, as is reported in authentic hadith. Also, the necessary thing is a part for the soul with a part of the body, any part.
Source pages p. 322

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the first objection against bodily resurrection. The objector argues using a cannibalism scenario: if one person eats another, the eaten person's parts become part of the eater, so God cannot restore both persons with the same parts. The author's answer is multi-layered. First, the marginal note points out that the scenario is unnecessary because in nature, corpses become soil and plants, etc. Second, the note suggests that resurrection may be by likeness, not exact identity, so the objection fails. Even if a child is born from those parts, God can prevent them from becoming original parts of the child, or the true original parts are taken by angels at death. The objection is adequately refuted.

The Objection of No Purpose and the Answer
Author refutes the objection that resurrection lacks a purpose by denying the premise.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that bodily resurrection lacks a purpose and how the author refutes it by challenging the assumption that the only possible purposes are pleasure or pain.

Core Idea
God's purposes are not limited to what we can conceive.

One of the strongest objections to bodily resurrection is that it seems pointless. If God resurrects us, what purpose does it serve? The objector forces a choice: either it's frivolous or it has a purpose. But the author shows this dilemma is false because we cannot restrict God's wisdom to our limited understanding. In this lesson, we walk through the objection and the author's decisive answer.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the logical structure: the objector presents a dilemma with two main branches and sub-branches.
Pay attention to the terms: غرض (purpose), عبث (frivolity), إيلام (infliction of pain), إلذاذ (bestowal of pleasure).
2
Understand the author's answer
The author responds by denying the premise that the only possible purposes are pleasure or pain, and by referencing a previous answer to the frivolity charge.
The author also addresses a subtle point about pleasure being the removal of pain.
3
Reflect on the broader principle
Consider why this objection fails: it assumes we can fully comprehend God's purposes. The lesson is that divine wisdom is beyond human limitation.
This connects to the core idea of the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني : لو حشر فإما لا لغرض وهو عبث لا يتصور في أفعاله تعالى ( وإما لغرض إما عائد إلى الله تعالى وهو منزه عنه أو إلى العبد وهو إما الإيلام وإنه منتف إجماعاً ) العقلاء ) وبديهة العقل أيضاً، وذلك لقبحه وعدم ملاءمته للحكمة) الإلهية والعناية) الأزلية ( وإما الإلذاذ وهو أيضاً باطل لأن اللذة) الجسمانية لا حقيقة لها ( إنما هو دفع الألم بالاستقراء وإنه لو ترك ( على حاله ولم يعد ( لم يكن له ألم )، فهذا الغرض حاصل بدون الإعادة فلا فائدة فيها، (و) إما ( الإيلام) أولا ( ليدفع) ذلك الألم ثانياً، (فيلتذ ) بعدمه فهو ( لا يصلح غرضاً إذ لا معنى له)، كان يمرض عبده ليدفعه عنه فيلتذ به أي يعود إلى عدم المرض . ( الجواب : نختار أنه لا لغرض وحكاية العبث والقبح العقلي قد مرّ جوابه ولا نسلم أن الغرض هو إما الإيلام أو الإلذاذ ولعل فيه غرضاً آخر لا نعلمه سلمنا أن الغرض منحصر فيهما (لكن لا
قوله : ( فلهذا الغرض حاصل بدون (الإعادة فيه بحث وهو أن اللذة على تقدير عدميتها انتفاع الألم لا انتفاؤه، ومن البين عدم حصوله على تقدير تركه على حاله، اللهم إلا أن يعتبر الألم حالة الحياة والاندفاع بالموت والعدم ولا يخفى بعده.
The second: If resurrection occurs, then either for no purpose—which is frivolity inconceivable in God's actions—or for a purpose. The purpose is either returning to God—He is exalted above that—or to the servant. The purpose to the servant is either infliction of pain—which is invalid by the consensus of rational people and by reason's intuition, due to its ugliness and incompatibility with divine wisdom and eternal care—or bestowal of pleasure, which is also false because bodily pleasure has no reality; it is merely the removal of pain by induction. And if he were left as he is and not resurrected, he would have no pain. So this purpose is obtained without resurrection, so there is no benefit in it. Or (the purpose is) infliction of pain first to remove it later, then he would experience pleasure from its absence. This does not serve as a purpose because it is meaningless—like making one's slave ill to cure him and then take pleasure in the return to health.
(The answer: We choose that there is no purpose, and the issue of frivolity and rational ugliness has already been answered. And we do not grant that the purpose is only infliction of pain or bestowal of pleasure. Perhaps there is another purpose that we do not know. Suppose we grant that the purpose is limited to these two, even then...
Statement: 'So this purpose is obtained without resurrection.' There is discussion about it: Pleasure, on the assumption of its non-existence, is the removal of pain, not the non-existence of pain. And it is evident that it is not obtained on the assumption of leaving him as he is, unless one considers pain during life and its removal by death and non-existence, but its remoteness is not hidden.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage presents and refutes the second objection against bodily resurrection. The objector argues that resurrection must have a purpose; if it has no purpose, it is frivolous, and if it has a purpose, it must be either for God (impossible) or for the servant. For the servant, the possible purposes are either the infliction of pain or the bestowal of pleasure. Both are rejected: pain is ugly and incompatible with divine wisdom, and pleasure has no reality because it is merely the removal of pain. Moreover, if there is no resurrection, the dead would have no pain, so the purpose of removing pain is already achieved. The author's answer is that the objector's assumption that the only possible purposes are these two is false; there may be other purposes unknown to us. Also, the frivolity objection has been answered elsewhere. The marginal note adds a subtle point about pleasure and pain.

Key Takeaway

Let this lesson remind you: His purposes are not limited to what you can conceive. Will you repent from judging His actions based on your limited understanding?
